After the shellacking taken by the Democratic Party at the hands of Ronald Reagan in ‘84, everything went out the window. New Deal and Great Society orthodoxy, the bedrock of Democratic thought for generations, were unceremoniously ejected in favor of workfare and private enterprise.

Having ensured the destruction of their own 50-year-long political project, the Democrats sought out a champion to execute their new vision. First they turned to Mario Cuomo, the Hamlet on the Hudson, who humbly and magnanimously declined to run. Then they turned to Gary Hart, who self-destructed, unable to keep his personal life out of the papers.

Finally, by process of elimination, they settled on Michael Stanley Dukakis. The outsider’s insider, the inscrutable automaton, the purest of technocrats, Americans have a difficult time remembering if he’s fiscally moderate and socially liberal or the other way around. Dukakis is going to have to define himself on the national stage while battling crises abroad and creeping partisanship at home to secure a second term and remake America in his managerial image.

Michael Dukakis is smart enough to know that he isn’t smart enough. His entire theory of politics is built around that concept.

When he needs to decide on a course of action, it’s never by instinct. He assembles a panel, a task force of people with positions that vary every which way, hearing out every single one of them before coming to a conclusion. He does not make bold strokes. He moves incrementally, making minor adjustments, tweaking and tuning until the final product is better than it was before. When something does not go his way, Dukakis does not yell. Throughout the past decade, he has seen people with their lives on the line because they lack adequate healthcare. All of this has only strengthened his faith in the administrative state as a force for positive good, and now he seeks results.
